Nick Haigh

HI everyone and thanks to Finchie for making this possible. To be honest I have not maintained much contact with people from school since we left save a few.

So a little about my journey. I completely failed my A-levels owing to spending far too much time sailing rather than schooling, though somehow, I managed to get into further education at Brighton Poly. That kept me in contact with Nick Carey and Guy Finned through university days and for a while after.

Having decided that the Engineering degree I got was not what I wanted I got into IT and moved from Brighton to the Farnborough/Camberley area. This is where I met my wife, Zoe, and we are celebrating our 30th anniversary this year.

After school I spent a lot of time with Mark Foulsham (happy birthday today!) and Guy Wood (RIP â˜¹ï¸). That was really the basis of my main social circle at that time.

In 1999 we had our first child, Elizabeth (now 23 and in Law School) and then in 2001 I decided to take a work opportunity and move the family to the States, landing on the East Coast in the New York Area. In 2005 I got into consulting and that took me all over the States and working with all sorts of companies. It is still what I do today but with much less travel. In 2008 we had a second child, Isabelle, who is now 15, failing High School, and who is likely to be Elizabeths first client!

Through my consulting days I was spending way too much time on the West Coast and so about 10 years ago we moved to the San Francisco Bay Area which is where I am now. Without a doubt the best part of the world I have lived in so far.

My memories of Reeds are mixed, some good mainly in the really stupid shit we got up to and some bad, mainly the education. I have often wondered what it would be like to be more connected to people from those days so maybe this is a chance.

I donâ€™t have much opportunity to get back to the UK but am looking to see if I can make this reunion work in some way.

Great to hear from you, Nick, and it sounds like a really interesting journey you have been on. Definitely get the mixed feelings thing about Reeds. I did keep in touch briefly with a few people after I left after 5th form, including Guy Wood, rest his soul. In my consultancy role, I work a lot with UK independent schools, and they have certainly changed. Good in ways, but they can't get away with the stuff we did and I guess that was why we remember so well the fun of firecrackers, crazy kitchen staff, slightly dodgy teachers, gozzing, playing rugby in heavy snow, Ma Holt's knickers. I remember selling raw leeks that I grew to starving boarders in the Close! Being caught coming out of the broom cupboard having found the passage down the WW2 air raid shelter. After 40 years, there are some great stories coming from everyone, and looking forward to catching up in June.

Peter Potts

I thoroughly enjoyed my time at Reed's School. I had a great rapport with the teachers and had a genuine interest in sports, although I had to bid farewell to rugby due to a string of injuries. Looking back, I realize how fortunate we were because California's private schools have relatively modest sports facilities. This led me to transfer my daughter to a public high school, which have impressive setups, although she still showed no interest in sports, which was a bit disappointing.

I scraped admission into Reed's School because of my exceptional math skills only. During that time, my father faced a life-threatening brain tumor with only a 25% chance of survival. We chose Reed's because they promised to keep me enrolled even if the worst happened. Fortunately, my father survived, albeit with some disabilities, and he is now 89 years old.

Over the years, I steadily climbed the academic ranks, eventually becoming a favorite among the teachers during my A-levels. Such was their trust in me that even when I consumed a third of a bottle of whiskey and had an unfortunate encounter with Hewitt's feet, I escaped any punishment. I think they considered the horrendous hangover the following day punishment enough.

I cherish memories of fireworks being launched from our dormitory windows and the time someone mischievously set one off inside the dormitory, causing it to zip around without causing any harm or starting a fire.

My performance at A-levels was outstanding, earning me a place at Cambridge to study Mathematics. However, there was a condition attached â€“ I had to pass an O-level in a foreign language. As a result, I extended my stay at Reed's for an additional year to study Latin; there was no way I was revisiting French.

During that extra year, I received a somewhat regal treatment, complete with my own room in Blathwayt. I even rigged up a sound system that would automatically turn off when the door opened. Poor Hewitt had a run-in with it when he attempted to reprimand me for playing music too loudly.

Upon arriving at Cambridge, I quickly realized I was lagging behind in Mathematics compared to my peers. While everyone else had studied Further Mathematics at A-level, Reed's had never offered that option. In my very first lecture at Cambridge, the professor urged us to forget everything we had learned in school because the real mathematical journey was commencing.

My time at Cambridge was a true eye-opener, offering a challenging and transformative experience. On the sports front, the hockey skills I acquired at Reed's proved incredibly valuable. I earned a spot in the St John's College first team for three years and even had the opportunity to play in the University 3rd team on one occasion.

I'm not quite sure what the outcome would have been had the trip to the US not prompted a visit to A&E. I got back on the field for the rest of that term once my arm had healed.

Graham Hales

So here's my round-up ... and apologies, it's taken a bit of time. First up it's great to see that you guys has such a good time in 6th form and embryonic friendships clearly deepened. I took a different path and bailed out post O-levels. Not really sure what drove that, but I do remember a conversation with @447896340167 where I was telling him the benefits of staying for 6th form and he was telling me why it was time to go. I don't know if it was that conversation or my general sense of teenage apathy, but either way I felt I needed a change of scene and a different journey from the one my brothers had so effortlessly thrived in, so off I went. I ended up at a local tech college and saw a very different side of life and then went on to do Business Studies. taking the heavy drinking option and it seems some of you took that course too! Realising I needed income, I went into advertising for around 10 years which enabled me to combine the drinking with something called work, which was all great fun. I then evolved into brand consulting and ended up as CEO of a company called Interbrand which took me all over the world and provided some great adventures. That all ended about 7 years ago and since then I've been doing some consultancy on my own, taken a few full time roles including time as CEO of a start-up consultancy, big fish, and I now work sporadically as a consultant, coach and volunteer a bit with the Samaritans. Along the way I was married for 20 years. That imploded spectacularly but I've not once regretted that ending! We had x3 kids, Violet 19, Sam 17 and Tilly 15 and I've now been with my new partner, MIchele, for the last 5 years. @447952310564 came by a few years ago which was great fun and brought back many happy memories. Anyway, in the spirit of Churchill wanting to write a short letter, but not having the time, I'll end it there. Delighted to see you're all doing so well from the notes unlocked by @16504609902. The pics are brilliant, maybe a few signs of lives well lived! Onwards!

The record that changed the world for a few people in my circle. I did two UK tours with Bam as support DJ, my friend Dapo was his manager in Europe at the time so he tapped me for it. 9/17/23, 8:28:45â€¯AM
Tom Hardiment

Well remembered Finchie. When he left Mr Williams replaced him. I did A level art with him. He was a useless teacher and a lazy fecker. He kept bunking off from teaching us to see his girlfriend in London. He asked us to cover for him each time but after a couple of terms we just said to Prince, when he put his head into the art studio, that he was in London. He was sacked the next week. His idea of teaching art was to say cut out a picture you like from a magazine, draw a grid over it scale it up and copy it. Peter Thomas was a friend of Mr Prince and stood in for the last two terms and saved us all from certain failure. Taught us to sculpt, screen print, throw pots; paint with oil, gouache and water colour and draw with conte, pastel and charcoal. Still life, life classes and from nature. Without the A for art A Level I wouldn't have got to uni. Whoever employed Mr Williams should've been sacked too.

Still in touch with Peter. He lives in the Dordogne and exhibits in London each year. Lovely guy.

In the studio we (Rob Lee, James G, Joe Moreton and Joe Chan, & Mrs Martin!) listened to Fleetwood Mac - Rumours, Rickie Lee Jones and Van Morrison - Astral Weeks, drank a lot of tea and dyed our hair with peroxide. Good times.

Peter Potts

If you read the book, one trick that active fund managers do, is to shutdown underperforming funds, so it always looks like they are beating the market. Also, high return requires high risk, low return comes from low risk, but if you average the saw tooth of high returns over economic cycles, you find that the overall returns are similar often. So, why stress yourself.

10/11/23, 8:14:27â€¯AM
Peter Potts

Another point to consider is that a reasonable return on an investment (after all expenses and taxes) over decades might be 4%. A typically active fund manager charges at least 1%. So, you are losing 25% of your profits right there before you even get out of the gate. â€

10/11/23, 8:18:39â€¯AM
Peter Potts
